<p><b>Charles&#160;V</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <span lang="es" xml:lang="es"><i>Carlos</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Karl</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <i>Karel</i>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Carlo</i></span>)<sup class="reference" id="ref_anone"><a href="#endnote_anone">[a]</a></sup> (24 February 1500 – 21 September 1558) was ruler of both the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> from 1516 and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> from 1519, as well as of the lands of the former <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">Duchy of Burgundy</a> from 1506. He voluntarily stepped down from these and other positions by a series of <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">abdications</a> between 1554 and 1556. Through inheritance, he brought together under his rule extensive territories in western, central, and southern Europe, and the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonies</a> in the Americas and Asia. As a result, his domains spanned nearly four million square kilometers<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and were the first to be described as "<a href="/wiki/The_empire_on_which_the_sun_never_sets" title="The empire on which the sun never sets">the empire on which the sun never sets</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-ThomasChesworth2015_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ThomasChesworth2015-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Charles was the heir of three of Europe's leading dynasties: the Houses of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Valois-Burgundy" title="House of Valois-Burgundy">Valois-Burgundy</a> (Burgundy and Netherlands), <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburg</a> (Holy Roman Empire), and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Trast%C3%A1mara" title="House of Trastámara">Trastámara</a> (Spain). He inherited the <a href="/wiki/Burgundian_Netherlands" title="Burgundian Netherlands">Burgundian Netherlands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Franche-Comt%C3%A9" title="Franche-Comté">Franche-Comté</a> as heir of the House of Valois-Burgundy. From his own dynasty, the Habsburgs, he inherited <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Austria and other lands in central Europe</a>. He was also elected to succeed his Habsburg grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>, a title held by the Habsburgs since 1440. From the Spanish House of Trastámara, he inherited the crowns of <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a>, which was in the process of developing a nascent empire in the Americas and Asia, and <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Crown of Aragon</a>, which included a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> empire extending to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">Southern Italy</a>. Charles was the first king to rule Castile and Aragon simultaneously in his own right and as a result he is sometimes referred to as the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spanish_monarchs" title="List of Spanish monarchs">king of Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MacCulloch2004_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacCulloch2004-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal union</a>, under Charles, of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> resulted in the closest Europe would come to a <a href="/wiki/Universal_monarchy" title="Universal monarchy">universal monarchy</a> since the death of <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a>.</p>
<p>Because of widespread fears that his vast inheritance would lead to the realization of a universal monarchy and that he was trying to create a European hegemony, Charles was the object of hostility from many enemies.<sup id="cite_ref-Berenger2014_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berenger2014-6">[6]</a></sup> His reign was dominated by war and particularly by three major simultaneous conflicts: the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg-Valois_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg-Valois Wars">Habsburg-Valois Wars</a> with France, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Habsburg_wars" title="Ottoman–Habsburg wars">struggle to halt the Ottoman advance</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> resulting in <a href="/wiki/European_wars_of_religion#The_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="European wars of religion">conflict with the German princes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maltby2002_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maltby2002-7">[7]</a></sup> The wars with France, mainly fought in Italy, resulted in recovery of territory lost at the beginning of his reign and included the decisive defeat and capture of <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I of France</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pavia" title="Battle of Pavia">Battle of Pavia</a> in 1525. France recovered and the wars continued for the remainder of Charles's reign. Enormously expensive, they led to the development of the first modern professional army in Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Tercios" class="mw-redirect" title="Tercios">Tercios</a>.</p>
<p>The struggle with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> was fought in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Hungary in the Middle Ages">Hungary</a> and the Mediterranean. After seizing most of eastern and central Hungary in 1526, the Ottomans' advance was halted at their failed <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vienna" title="Siege of Vienna">Siege of Vienna</a> in 1529. <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Habsburg_wars_in_Hungary_(1526%E2%80%931568)" title="Ottoman–Habsburg wars in Hungary (1526–1568)">A lengthy war</a> of attrition, conducted on his behalf by his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand</a> (who had been elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_Hungarian_monarchs" title="List of Hungarian monarchs">king of Hungary</a>), continued for the rest of Charles's reign. In the Mediterranean, although there were some successes, Charles was unable to prevent the Ottomans' increasing naval dominance and the piratical activity of the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary corsairs</a>. Charles opposed the Reformation and in Germany he was in conflict with the Protestant Princes of the <a href="/wiki/Schmalkaldic_League" title="Schmalkaldic League">Schmalkaldic League</a> who were motivated by both religious and political opposition to him. He could not prevent the spread of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> and although he won a decisive victory against the Princes at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_M%C3%BChlberg" title="Battle of Mühlberg">Battle of Mühlberg</a>, 1547, he was ultimately forced to concede the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Augsburg" title="Peace of Augsburg">Peace of Augsburg</a> of 1555, which divided Germany on confessional lines.</p>
<p>While Charles did not typically concern himself with rebellions, he was quick to put down three particularly dangerous rebellions in the vital territories of <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Comuneros" title="Revolt of the Comuneros">Castile</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arumer_Zwarte_Hoop" title="Arumer Zwarte Hoop">Frisian lands</a>, and later in his reign in the city of <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_Ghent_(1539)" title="Revolt of Ghent (1539)">Ghent</a>. Once the rebellions were quelled the essential Castilian and Burgundian territories remained mostly loyal to Charles throughout his rule.</p>
<p>Charles's Spanish dominions were the chief source of his power and wealth, and they became increasingly important as his reign progressed. In the Americas, Charles sanctioned the conquest by Castillian <i><a href="/wiki/Conquistadors" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquistadors">conquistadores</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire">Aztec</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire">Inca</a> empires. Castillian control was extended across much of South and Central America. The resulting vast expansion of territory and the flows of South American silver to Castile had profound long term effects on Spain.</p>
<p>Charles was only 56 when he abdicated, but after 40 years of energetic rule he was physically exhausted and sought the peace of a monastery, where he died at the age of 58. Upon Charles's abdications, the Holy Roman Empire was inherited by his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand</a>, who had already been given the Austrian lands in 1521. The Spanish Empire, including the possessions in the Netherlands and Italy, was inherited by Charles's son <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a>. The two empires would remain allies until the 18th century (when the Spanish branch of the House of Habsburg became extinct).</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage_and_early_life">Heritage and early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Heritage and early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2984" data-file-height="4161" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barend_van_Orley_-_Portrait_of_Charles_V_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_van_Orley" title="Bernard van Orley">Bernard van Orley</a>, 1519</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Charles was born in 1500 as the eldest son of <a href="/wiki/Philip_I_of_Castile" title="Philip I of Castile">Philip the Handsome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joanna_of_Castile" title="Joanna of Castile">Joanna of Castile</a> in the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Flanders" title="County of Flanders">Flemish</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a>, which was part of the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Netherlands" title="Habsburg Netherlands">Habsburg Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-State2015_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-State2015-8">[8]</a></sup> The culture and courtly life of the <a href="/wiki/Burgundian_Low_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgundian Low Countries">Burgundian Low Countries</a> were an important influence in his early life. He was tutored by <a href="/wiki/William_de_Cro%C3%BF" title="William de Croÿ">William de Croÿ</a> (who would later become his first prime minister), and also by Adrian of Utrecht (later <a href="/wiki/Pope_Adrian_VI" title="Pope Adrian VI">Pope Adrian VI</a>). It is said that Charles spoke several vernacular languages: he was fluent in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, later adding an acceptable <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Castilian Spanish</a> (which Charles called the "divine language"<sup id="cite_ref-Wilkens1897_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkens1897-9">[9]</a></sup>) required by the <a href="/wiki/Old_Castile" title="Old Castile">Castilian</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Cortes_Generales" title="Cortes Generales">Cortes Generales</a></i> as a condition for becoming <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">King of Castile</a>. He also gained a decent command of <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> (in which he was not fluent prior to his election), though he never spoke it as well as French.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> A witticism sometimes attributed to Charles is: "I speak Spanish to God, Italian to women, French to men and German to my horse." A variant of the quote is attributed to him by Swift in his 1726 <i><a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver's Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a></i>, but there are many other variants and it is often attributed instead to <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>From his Burgundian ancestors he inherited an ambiguous relationship with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_French_monarchs" title="List of French monarchs">Kings of France</a>. Charles shared with France his mother tongue and many cultural forms. In his youth he made frequent visits to Paris, then the largest city of Western Europe. In his words: "Paris is not a city, but a universe" (<i><a href="/wiki/Lutetia" title="Lutetia">Lutetia</a> non urbs, sed orbis</i>). He was betrothed to both Louise and <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_of_Valois" class="mw-redirect" title="Charlotte of Valois">Charlotte of Valois</a>, daughters of King <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I of France</a>, but they both died in childhood. Charles also inherited the tradition of political and dynastic enmity between the royal and the Burgundian ducal lines of the <a href="/wiki/Valois_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Valois dynasty">Valois dynasty</a>. Charles was very attached to the Burgundian Low Countries where he had been raised. These lands were very rich and contributed significantly to the wealth of the Empire. He also spent much time there, mainly in Brussels. This stands in contrast with the attitude of his son Philip who only visited the Low Countries once.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg/170px-Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg" width="170" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg/255px-Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg/340px-Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1001" data-file-height="1670" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jakob_Seisenegger_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Seisenegger" title="Jakob Seisenegger">Jakob Seisenegger</a>, 1533</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Until the 1540s, Charles did not spend much time in Germany (apart from the Netherlands). He frequently was in Northern Italy (then part of the Holy Roman Empire of German Nation). He never actually governed his Austrian dominions and made his brother <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I_Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferdinand I Habsburg">Ferdinand</a> the ruler of these lands in 1521, as well as his representative in the Holy Roman Empire during his absence. In spite of this, the Emperor had a close relationship with some German families, like the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Nassau" title="House of Nassau">House of Nassau</a>, many of which were represented at his court in Brussels. Some German princes or noblemen accompanied him in his military campaigns against France or the Ottomans, and the bulk of his army was generally composed of German troops, especially the Imperial <a href="/wiki/Landsknechte" class="mw-redirect" title="Landsknechte">Landsknechte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indeed, in 1519, he was elected because he was considered a German prince while his main opponent was French. Nonetheless, in the long term, the growth of Lutheranism and Charles' staunch Catholicism alienated him from various German princes who finally fought against him in the 1540s and the 1550s. It is important to note, though, that other states of the Empire chose to support him in his war, and that he had the constant support of his brother, in spite of their strained personal relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Whereas Charles spent much of his final years as a ruler trying to address the issue of religion in the Empire, it would ultimately be Ferdinand, by then much more popular in Germany, who would bring peace to the German lands.</p>
<p>Though Spain was the core of his personal possessions and though he had many Iberian ancestors, in his earlier years Charles felt as if he were viewed as a foreign prince. He became fluent in Spanish late in his life, as it was not his first language. Nonetheless, he spent much of his life in Spain, including his final years in a Spanish monastery, and his heir, later Philip II, was born and raised in Spain. Indeed, Charles's motto, <i><a href="/wiki/Plus_Ultra_(motto)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plus Ultra (motto)">Plus Ultra</a></i> ('Further Beyond'), became the national motto of Spain. He had many Spanish counselors and, except for the revolt of the comuneros in the 1520s, Spain remained mostly loyal to him. Spain was also his most important military asset, as it provided a great number of generals, as well as the formidable Spanish <a href="/wiki/Tercios" class="mw-redirect" title="Tercios">tercios</a>, considered the best infantry of its time. Many Spaniards, however, believed that their resources were being used to sustain a policy that was not in the country's interest.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> They usually believed that Charles should have focused on the Mediterranean and North Africa instead of Northern or Central Europe.</p>

</div>
